  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_0b2abc12-762a-4f90-a220-30b05e510bc9'
      });
    }

It is perhaps of little to no real relevance with respect to his exceptional legacy of creative writing ability... but, given his astonishing iconic insight, did Tom Sharpe ever visit, or live, in S. Africa?</a>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_2414443496" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2414443496" />
    <a title="Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/78485297-mario-the-lone-bookwolf-semi-reviewing-hiatus"><img alt="Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588320656p2/78485297.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2414443496?book_show_action=true">Jun 23, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) " href="/user/show/78485297-mario-the-lone-bookwolf-semi-reviewing-hiatus">Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/78485297-mario-the-lone-bookwolf-semi-reviewing-hiatus?shelf=sharpe-tom">sharpe-tom</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2414443496" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7025958560216071999">Sharpe´s first novel is one of the darkest, most politically incorrect and at the same time important, sarcastic masterpieces, satirizing the inhumanity of South Africa´s Apartheid era.<br/> <br/>There are no sympathetic characters in this work, close to everyone except for one rare abnormity, is a racist, brutal, and self righteous antagonist, producing running gag incompetence based dangers for themselves and everyone around them while executing racist laws. Not sure if this is still funny or already mo</span>
  <span id="freeText7025958560216071999" style="display:none">Sharpe´s first novel is one of the darkest, most politically incorrect and at the same time important, sarcastic masterpieces, satirizing the inhumanity of South Africa´s Apartheid era.<br /> <br />There are no sympathetic characters in this work, close to everyone except for one rare abnormity, is a racist, brutal, and self righteous antagonist, producing running gag incompetence based dangers for themselves and everyone around them while executing racist laws. Not sure if this is still funny or already more of a tragedy, the laughing doesn´t feel right or good, it´s more a reflex out of the long time conditioning for black humor in my case, or a first reflex after realizing what is going on, after the general comedy tropes are used in such an unused and out of context way.<br /> <br />The sexual content seems a bit over the top, I guess Sharpe added it to give it the extra <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[interracial <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> provocation to produce even more outcries than with his other monstrous protagonists. Although I guess that there are people out there that may be more offended by the violence and naughty elements than by the satirized system.<br /> <br />One could not sell such a work nowadays, because misunderstood and misled political correctness makes it impossible to publish something with extremely drastic content to show how cruelty has long been an essential element of a governmental system. <br /> <br />Don´t get me wrong, emancipation and equality is the way to roll, but avoiding full explicit descriptions of how racists think, limits the options for showing the inherent evil of all kind of prejudices. Although just certain audiences should read such works, because everyone faint of hearth won´t be able to deal with this unique, very long introspectives into the minds formed out of something seen far too often in human history. Seriously, if you can´t handle extreme satire, better don´t read it, it will just disturb or disgust you.<br /> <br />I will certainly read the second part<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/58514.Indecent_Exposure">https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5...</a><br />that even includes some element of making fun of fringe humanities, something I do always appreciate.<br /><br />Tropes show how literature is conceptualized and created and which mixture of elements makes works and genres unique: <br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://tvtropes.org/pmwiki/pmwiki.php/Literature/RiotousAssembly">https://tvtropes.org/pmwiki/pmwiki.ph...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="7025958560216071999" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2455959580" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2455959580" />
    <a title="Glenn Russell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23385697-glenn-russell"><img alt="Glenn Russell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1505195595p2/23385697.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2455959580?book_show_action=true">Jul 16, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Glenn Russell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Glenn Russell" href="/user/show/23385697-glenn-russell">Glenn Russell</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2455959580" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15621448466441875684"><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1531548023i/25972208._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Sidesplitting, rib-tickling, rollicking. <i>Riotous Assembly</i> was my first exposure to Tom Sharpe. As I was reading I asked myself: who does this author remind me of? Then it hit me. Of course! None other than Mad Magazine’s maddest artist, Don Martin. I mean, take a look at the above illustration for this Tom Sharpe novel and the two Don Martin cartoons below. No doubt about it – Tom Sharpe and Don Martin share much of the same outrageous, over-the-top, darkish, rubbery sense of humor. <br/><br/>Don Martin w</span>
  <span id="freeText15621448466441875684" style="display:none"><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1531548023i/25972208._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Sidesplitting, rib-tickling, rollicking. <i>Riotous Assembly</i> was my first exposure to Tom Sharpe. As I was reading I asked myself: who does this author remind me of? Then it hit me. Of course! None other than Mad Magazine’s maddest artist, Don Martin. I mean, take a look at the above illustration for this Tom Sharpe novel and the two Don Martin cartoons below. No doubt about it – Tom Sharpe and Don Martin share much of the same outrageous, over-the-top, darkish, rubbery sense of humor. <br><br>Don Martin was my absolute favorite back when I was a kid. I gobbled up all his gleeful slapstick. So I suppose it is no surprise I took an instant liking to wacky, waggish Tom Sharpe. However, I certainly can appreciate such zaniness and imbecility, frequently dark in the extreme, is not for everyone. As one British critic commented: “If getting a taxidermist to stuff your grandfather and blowing up a neighbor’s house by pumping gas up his lavatory pan are your taste in jokes, then Mr. Sharpe is your man. If not, not.” <br><br>A bucketful of differences, obviously, between a panel cartoon and a novel but I so wish Don Martin worked on an illustrated edition of <i>Riotous Assembly</i> with its scathing satire revolving around South African apartheid, torture and executions complements of a police force and lurid sexual perversions complements of an old lady. Instant collector’s item.<br><br>But probably not a collector’s item in South Africa, at least among government officials back in 1971 when the novel was first published since Tom Sharpe got himself kicked out of the country some years prior for his anti-apartheid writing. Also worth noting, <i>Riotous Assembly</i> is the first of two novels targeting South African society, the country’s police force in particular, <i>Indecent Exposure</i> the second. In years thereafter, Tom Sharpe took aim at English society. <br><br>Back on <i>Riotous Assembly</i>. We’re in small, sleepy Piemburg, a South African city described by a visitor from the US as “Half the size of New York Cemetery and twice as dead.” Because so much of comedy is bound up with individual personality, author Tom treats us to an entire lineup of screwball characters. Oh, yes, even sluggish, slumbering Piemberg has its share. Among their number: <br><br>Kommandant van Heerden: Bumbling incompetent chief of police, stanch supporter of apartheid South Africa and lover of England and all things English. He’s at the mansion of wealthy English heiress Miss Hazelstone to deal with a sticky situation: the frail old lady admits to using a quadruple-barreled elephant gun to blow her colored cook Fivepense to smithereens. As she also reveals, Fivepense was her lover for the past eight years. <br><br>The Kommandant gives the necessary orders to hush up such a society-shattering, preposterous revelation, after all, the honor of Piemburg is at stake. If van Heerden only knew he would soon be stuck in a sticky situation of his own, as in dangling from a second story window, handcuffed to a bed, wearing a rubber mask and woman’s nightgown. Loony? Deranged? Welcome to the world of Tom Sharpe. <br><br>Konstabel Els: An absolute expert in operating his Electrical Therapy Machine to extract confessions. “His natural aptitude for violence and particularly for shooting black people was only equaled by his taste for brandy and his predilection for forcing the less attractive parts of his person into those parts of African women legally reserved for male members of their own race.” Just the man to guard Miss Hazlestone’s estate under van Heerden directions to shoot to kill. Els takes his job seriously, relishing every blast from his rifle. The result: 125 dead police. Good going Els! Now the Kommandant has a true catastrophe on his buffoonish hands. He might even be demoted. <br><br>Luitenant Verkramp: After receiving his orders from Kommandant van Heerden, this conscientious police officer leads an army to Miss Hazelstone’s estate, complete with a column of lorries and armored cars as well as signs announcing Bubonic Plague and an outbreak of Rabies. Unfortunately, Konstabel Els will take him for an enemy of the state. Or, is that enemy of the estate? In either case, Verkramp and his army will not return in one piece, and that’s understatement. <br><br>Sergeant de Kock: Exactly what is needed at the home of Miss Hazelstone – another officer of the law. As author Tom Sharpe writes: “The Sergeant was by no means a squeamish man and not in the least averse to shooting women. Plenty of Zulu widowers could attest to that.” As soon as the Sergeant arrives on the scene he lets off a volley from his gun aimed at the sky that results in his being covered in the feathers and guts of a very well-fed vulture (recall all those dead police). And there’s more high jink and horseplay afoot for the Sergeant, enough that, a the very least, he stands a good chance of getting shot himself. <br><br>Miss Hazlestone: Would you believe this little old lady orchestrates a reenactment of a decisive battle between a redcoat English infantry and Zulu warriors on the parade-ground of an insane asylum where hundreds of mental patients, black and white, participate with great enthusiasm? After all, what’s the harm since the all those hopped up Zulus are armed with only rubber spears. Wait a minute – those spears might be real! The spectators for this event could be in store for a bit of bloody bloodshed with all the crazies turned loose on one another. Is there anybody in the house not completely nuts? <br><br>Jonathan Hazelstone, Bishop of Barotseland: The fall guy. It's not long before the Bishop is being marched out to the gallows to be hanged. And to think, his execution could be part of well thought out plan to take his healthy English heart as a transplant for someone who truly needs it: none other than the one and only Kommandant van Heerden. The ups and downs, mostly downs, of Jonathan are among the more hysterically funny parts of the novel. <br><br>From <i>The Guardian</i>: "Sharpe was keen on the idea of both writing and reading as fun." After reading <i>Riotous Assembly</i>, I certainly concur. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1531548310i/25972216._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>British novelist Tom Sharpe, 1928-2013<br><br>"'It's someone who dresses up in rubber nighties and hangs out of other people's bedroom windows soliciting people below.' continued the Sergeant plucking feathers and lights off his uniform. 'It's also a product of the permissive society and as you all know South Africa is not a permissive society. What this swine is doing is against the law here, and what I suggest is that we shove a bullet or two up his arse and give him the thrill to end all thrills.'" - Tom Sharpe, <i>Riotous Assembly</i><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1531629240i/25976348._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1531631379i/25976383._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="15621448466441875684" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10340381340974996649">This book is simply hysterical. It is difficult to recommend to people as they tend to give you a very strange look when you explain that it is a satire of apartheid South Africa written by a white South African. This book is an exemplary piece of modern satire. The first time I read it, I was commuting on a public train and was laughing out loud the entire time. My mother and I would read it out loud to each other and laugh so hard we cried. In some parts it was impossible to read aloud because</span>
  <span id="freeText10340381340974996649" style="display:none">This book is simply hysterical. It is difficult to recommend to people as they tend to give you a very strange look when you explain that it is a satire of apartheid South Africa written by a white South African. This book is an exemplary piece of modern satire. The first time I read it, I was commuting on a public train and was laughing out loud the entire time. My mother and I would read it out loud to each other and laugh so hard we cried. In some parts it was impossible to read aloud because we were laughing so hard we couldn't breathe. I read the sequel but was disappointed. What was razor sharp criticism dulled to caricature - I would advise sticking with this book and avoiding the sequel. (for the record, Sharpe was eventually exiled from South Africa as a result of his criticism of the government)<br /><br /> </span>

<div id="review_644066729" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/644066729" />
    <a title="Monica" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5870323-monica"><img alt="Monica" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/644066729?book_show_action=true">Jun 17, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Monica" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Monica" href="/user/show/5870323-monica">Monica</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5870323-monica?shelf=reread">reread</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer644066729" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer969597952609671508">Tom Sharpe died last week and it moved me to go back and reread him. And boy does he hold up well. This book, written in 1970, is a brilliant, scathing, savagely funny look at South Africa under Apartheid. If P. G. Wodehouse and Evelyn Waugh had made a baby author, he might have turned out to be somebody like Tom Sharpe - making astute political criticism in a voice that is utterly irreverent, perverse and hilarious.<br/><br/>The plot is absurdly wonderful - an elderly British gentlewoman calls the local </span>
  <span id="freeText969597952609671508" style="display:none">Tom Sharpe died last week and it moved me to go back and reread him. And boy does he hold up well. This book, written in 1970, is a brilliant, scathing, savagely funny look at South Africa under Apartheid. If P. G. Wodehouse and Evelyn Waugh had made a baby author, he might have turned out to be somebody like Tom Sharpe - making astute political criticism in a voice that is utterly irreverent, perverse and hilarious.<br /><br />The plot is absurdly wonderful - an elderly British gentlewoman calls the local police to report that she has shot her Zulu cook in a crime of passion. The responding officers are Kommandant Van Heerden, a dim, hypochondriacal Anglophile, Lieutenant Verkramp, a rabid anti-Communist, and Konstabel Els, a trigger happy sadist, rapist and all around active agent of entropy. The woman, Miss Hazelstone, is the heiress of a family distinguished for being honored in spite of their wretched performance of duty, and she herself is, well, a transvestite with a latex fetish. Mayhem ensues.<br /><br />There are so many laugh out loud moments, that you almost, but not quite, forget the nightmare that was Apartheid.<br /><br />"There didn't seem to be any significant difference between life in the mental hospital and life in South Africa as a whole. Black madmen did all the work while white lunatics lounged around and imagined that they were God."</span>
  <a data-text-id="969597952609671508" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1226708638" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1226708638" />
    <a title="Andrew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/800291-andrew"><img alt="Andrew" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1295167791p2/800291.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1226708638?book_show_action=true">Mar 13, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andrew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrew" href="/user/show/800291-andrew">Andrew</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1226708638" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9420484425342998599">I have always loved the work of Tom Sharpe (I constantly regret the day I decided not to see him talk at our local library). The man had such a statical and biting wit born from his own person experiences. This book (and its sequel) are perfect examples of his own experiences shaping a story and personally I feel giving it far more relevance and weight for it. <br/><br/>Normally I would try and shy away from commenting about the story, however I can say that the description of this book (the "blurb") pret</span>
  <span id="freeText9420484425342998599" style="display:none">I have always loved the work of Tom Sharpe (I constantly regret the day I decided not to see him talk at our local library). The man had such a statical and biting wit born from his own person experiences. This book (and its sequel) are perfect examples of his own experiences shaping a story and personally I feel giving it far more relevance and weight for it. <br /><br />Normally I would try and shy away from commenting about the story, however I can say that the description of this book (the "blurb") pretty much covers off the plot line, what makes it so much fun is the sheer farcical story, it reads like one set up situation after another. Now normally this would be fun in its own right but Mr Sharpe spent many years living in South Africa at the height of apartheid and was eventually deported from there for speaking out one too many times against it. A lot of the situations although not real where the creation of experiences and personalities he witnessed while working there as a teacher and social worker.<br /><br />So the truth strength of Tom Sharpe's work, taking aspects of our society and turning them in to a farce yet at the same time not degrading or ignoring the people wrapped up them. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9420484425342998599" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

It was a little bit predictable as far as who was going to come out on top, but since the element of suspense is beside the point of the novel, no complaints. <span id="freeTextContainer15848980863598037788">This author gets my Big Brass Balls Award. Not many authors writing comedy would go near the issue of apartheid and racism in South Africa. It's not a funny topic. But Sharpe's depictions of rednecks, racists, and bigots are as brutal as the crimes they have committed against humanity. Sharpe is an overlooked author and this is where you should start. One of a kind. </span>

          </span>

<div id="review_1128193893" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1128193893" />
    <a title="Tom Cöle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22076949-tom-c-le"><img alt="Tom Cöle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1373451902p2/22076949.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1128193893?book_show_action=true">Dec 10, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tom Cöle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tom Cöle" href="/user/show/22076949-tom-c-le">Tom Cöle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1128193893" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5198273716215253439">Pure hilarity from beginning to end. A delicious blend of satire and farce shot through with caricature-like characterisation, dazzling wit and healthy dollops of smut. Manages to send up and satirise the whole history of South Africa up to the apartheid era and still contain scenes of cartoon violence featuring policemen and attack dogs squeezing each other's knackers. Kommandant van Heerden, Konstabel Els and Lieutenant Verkramp - the book's central characters - are all amusingly flawed bonehe</span>
  <span id="freeText5198273716215253439" style="display:none">Pure hilarity from beginning to end. A delicious blend of satire and farce shot through with caricature-like characterisation, dazzling wit and healthy dollops of smut. Manages to send up and satirise the whole history of South Africa up to the apartheid era and still contain scenes of cartoon violence featuring policemen and attack dogs squeezing each other's knackers. Kommandant van Heerden, Konstabel Els and Lieutenant Verkramp - the book's central characters - are all amusingly flawed boneheads and make up an oddly Hancockian trio for a threesome of apartheid-enforcing Afrikaners, while other highlights in the cast include a mad, aged rubber-fetisihist spinster, a drunken clergyman, and a lisping lawyer with a high opinion of himself. <br /><br />I laughed and laughed and laughed the length of the Central Line while reading this and would recommend it to anyone, especially to fans of William Donaldson's comic novels, which this rather reminded me of. A triumph!<br /><br />(Also, happily the Kommandant and his men are the central characters in the sequel, Indecent Exposure, too...)</span>
  <a data-text-id="5198273716215253439" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer235688078" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18202597134589528778">This book is a masterpiece of satire and slapstick humour set in Apartheid South Africa. With the release of Mandela and the fall of Apartheid it is possibly less funny and less relevant. The plot is frankly ludicrous, the characters absurdly drawn (or so I thought until a Boer told me I was "a waste of God's good oxygen" three years ago) and his attention to detail - the use of local anaesthetic injections to prevent premature ejaculation, for example - is masterful. The books written around th</span>
  <span id="freeText18202597134589528778" style="display:none">This book is a masterpiece of satire and slapstick humour set in Apartheid South Africa. With the release of Mandela and the fall of Apartheid it is possibly less funny and less relevant. The plot is frankly ludicrous, the characters absurdly drawn (or so I thought until a Boer told me I was "a waste of God's good oxygen" three years ago) and his attention to detail - the use of local anaesthetic injections to prevent premature ejaculation, for example - is masterful. The books written around this time a Sharpe's zenith as a satirist.</span>

<div id="review_451682932" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/451682932" />
    <a title="Will Wraxall" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13985127-will-wraxall"><img alt="Will Wraxall" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601806723p2/13985127.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/451682932?book_show_action=true">Nov 08, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Will Wraxall" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Will Wraxall" href="/user/show/13985127-will-wraxall">Will Wraxall</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer451682932" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8228954324917988289">Warning for mild spoilers...<br/><br/>Brilliant, brilliant satire of segregation in South Africa, and especially the useless and deplorable South African police force. The first half of the book is pure farcical genius, like Fawlty Towers in literary form as a seemingly straightforward prolem develops slowly but surely into all-out siege warfare. The characters are broadly sketched, as you would expect from a comedy, but they are utterly unforgettable; the misguided and dim-witted racist Kommandant van He</span>
  <span id="freeText8228954324917988289" style="display:none">Warning for mild spoilers...<br /><br />Brilliant, brilliant satire of segregation in South Africa, and especially the useless and deplorable South African police force. The first half of the book is pure farcical genius, like Fawlty Towers in literary form as a seemingly straightforward prolem develops slowly but surely into all-out siege warfare. The characters are broadly sketched, as you would expect from a comedy, but they are utterly unforgettable; the misguided and dim-witted racist Kommandant van Heerden, the paranoid Luitenant Verkramp, and the utter comic nirvana that is the violently inept and psychotic Konstabel Els. In fact the only real problem with Tom Sharpe's debut is that the first half is so sublime, the second half can't quite live up to it - the plot loses a bit of momentum, there are one or two scenes that don't feel like they add a great deal and the comedy becomes slightly more sporadically inspired rather than consistently pitch perfect. Having said that, it's still searingly and savagely funnier than just about anything else you'll read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8228954324917988289" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/931353716?book_show_action=true">May 08, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mark Speed" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mark Speed" href="/user/show/23382243-mark-speed">Mark Speed</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23382243-mark-speed?shelf=satire">satire</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23382243-mark-speed?shelf=contemporary-literature">contemporary-literature</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer931353716" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10098532717296921334">When I was 17 the first chapter of this was read to us in class. We fell about laughing at the imbecility of the South African police characters.<br/><br/>At that time, the Apartheid regime in South Africa - against whom this satire was tilted - was hardening against resistance. Amazingly, on initial publication in the US, the novel had been boycotted over its dedication:<br/><br/>"For all those members of the South African Police Force whose lives are dedicated to the preservation of Western Civilisation in Southe</span>
  <span id="freeText10098532717296921334" style="display:none">When I was 17 the first chapter of this was read to us in class. We fell about laughing at the imbecility of the South African police characters.<br /><br />At that time, the Apartheid regime in South Africa - against whom this satire was tilted - was hardening against resistance. Amazingly, on initial publication in the US, the novel had been boycotted over its dedication:<br /><br />"For all those members of the South African Police Force whose lives are dedicated to the preservation of Western Civilisation in Southern Africa"<br /><br />Some readers evidently thought it was a serious dedication, rather than a sarcastic pop at the regime. Sharpe was deported from South Africa for sedition in 1961.<br /><br />I reread it a few years ago. It stands up well, but I guess the butt of the joke should now be a corrupt ANC, whose leaders have lined their pockets well. Alas, South African politics now resembles <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7613.Animal_Farm" title="Animal Farm by George Orwell" rel="nofollow noopener">Animal Farm</a>...</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer143432739902739849">This is the third time I've read this and it just keeps on getting both funnier and more relevant. This book could be dismissed as a racist lampoon of Apartheid but under the surface is a complex and ruthless honesty about the attitudes that allowed Apartheid to happen and continue for as long as it did. This is a merciless satire also full of the usual side-splitting farcical events that had me laughing out loud on several occasions and nodding in agreement at others.<br/>Crude and rude and not for </span>
  <span id="freeText143432739902739849" style="display:none">This is the third time I've read this and it just keeps on getting both funnier and more relevant. This book could be dismissed as a racist lampoon of Apartheid but under the surface is a complex and ruthless honesty about the attitudes that allowed Apartheid to happen and continue for as long as it did. This is a merciless satire also full of the usual side-splitting farcical events that had me laughing out loud on several occasions and nodding in agreement at others.<br />Crude and rude and not for the easily offended, this is one of the best satires I have ever read.</span>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6280542620350615744">"In poor taste"; "shocking"; "grossly offensive"; "obscene" - Tom Sharpe's first novel may be described thus. Yet for Sharpe, I'm sure these reviews would be considered high praise, and the mark of a job well done. This book put the crass absurdity of South African Apartheid into vivid perspective, while also providing a few laughs. I highly doubt anyone would dare publish it today. <br/><br/>JM</span>

<div id="review_2782563356" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2782563356" />
    <a title="Realini" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7873218-realini"><img alt="Realini" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1420269105p2/7873218.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2782563356?book_show_action=true">Apr 11, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Realini" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Realini" href="/user/show/7873218-realini">Realini</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7873218-realini?shelf=humor">humor</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2782563356" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9644313019406033922">Riotous Assembly by Tom Sharpe<br/>9 out of 10<br/><br/><br/>Having been thrilled and exalted by Blott on the Landscape- included on the 1,000 Novels Everyone Must Read List - I have decided to read other works by the extremely talented Tom Sharpe and hence the encounter with his first work, Riotous Assembly.<br/><br/>The beginning of the book makes clear the enjoyment provided for the reader, with the description of the small, fictional town in South Africa, Piemburg, where the Riotous Assembly gathers:<br/><br/>"A place half the siz</span>
  <span id="freeText9644313019406033922" style="display:none">Riotous Assembly by Tom Sharpe<br />9 out of 10<br /><br /><br />Having been thrilled and exalted by Blott on the Landscape- included on the 1,000 Novels Everyone Must Read List - I have decided to read other works by the extremely talented Tom Sharpe and hence the encounter with his first work, Riotous Assembly.<br /><br />The beginning of the book makes clear the enjoyment provided for the reader, with the description of the small, fictional town in South Africa, Piemburg, where the Riotous Assembly gathers:<br /><br />"A place half the size of a New York cemetery and twice as dead"<br /><br />Kommandant van Heerden is one of the key figures of the story, an admirer of the British, who hopes and eventually thinks he gets an English heart, for he admires the British, albeit the plot of the novel would make him see the English involved in outrageous acts, some of them imagined, others only atrocious from the point of view of this peculiar character.<br />He is called to deal with a reported shooting, the British spinster Miss Hazlestone calls to report having killed her black cook, Sixpence.<br /><br />Unaware that six pence is the nickname of the victim, the police dismiss the call and they insist that the killing of what they call a "coon" does not count, for the brilliant novel is much more than a comedy, it exposes the brutality and stupidity of apartheid, a ghastly regime in which the lives of the native majority did not count.<br />When the Kommandant arrives at the Jacaranda House, he tries to explain to the woman who has reported the murder of the cook that she has done the right thing, for black people have been forbidden access in the area and she was within her rights, and he would not budge, even when the woman insists that the cook has had every right to enter the house since he was employed there.<br /><br />Furthermore, Van Heerden is flabbergasted to learn that the dead Sixpence has been the lover of the white woman and this is a "crime passionnel", or crime of passion, as she has to translate to the horrified man of the law, who has been dismissing the death of the cook as being irrelevant...no white person has ever been convicted for killing a native man or woman.<br />The murder weapon is an outre elephant gun, with no less than four barrels, designed by the late father of the eccentric woman, and it has vaporized, obliterated the dead lover.<br /><br />Details of the affair are revealed, adding to the torment of the white supremacist chief of police, who listens with terror as the killer explains that she had so much in common with the victim, they both loved rubber, although the man suffered from ejaculatio precox, a term explained to the interlocutor, with vivid images of the servant who ejaculated as soon as he was touched by his mistress.<br />To cope with this issue, the inventive woman made him use two rubbers, taking one out after one hour and the other later, so that they reach simultaneous orgasms, a goal achieved with another stimulant, injections with Novocain in his penis.<br /><br />We can imagine the panic, hearat trouble of the Kommandant when he hears more...for cook and Miss Hazlestone played roles, she was dressing as a man and he was using women's clothing.<br />Avoiding spoilers, I will just mention that the chief of police himself would be in a restrained position, handcuffed to the bed and incapacitated, when the inventive, lurid, intrepid Miss Hazlestone dresses him in women's clothing and somehow, trying to escape while she is fighting an incoming group of attackers, the Kommandant finds himself hanging from the window and abused by men of the law, who do not recognize him.<br /><br />South Africa of this story is not just racist, nationalist, but also very vicious with gays, transvestism and when they see this man, they abuse him and are determined to shoot and kill him, for his perversion- as they look at it - has no cure and there can be no tolerance.<br />When the desperate chief of police tries to inform them that he is not to be touched, they add to the crime of perversion, that of impersonating an official...<br />What is in the first place a crime of passion, in the process of being covered up by the white police, turns into a calamity with the involvement of Konstabel Els, the idiot, cruel assistant of the chief of police.<br /><br />The plot is complicated when the brother of the white woman is found drunk and naked in the Jacaranda House and he becomes the principal suspect, the Anglophile chief of police is sure that this the way the honorable British try to hide the truth, to preserve respectability.<br />However, Jonathan Hazlestone is a bishop and not guilty of anything, expect getting drunk.<br /><br />When the investigation contacts the clergy and get an answer with the periods when Jonathan Hazlestone has been in different posts, the stupid officials take this to mean the different prison terms served in jails.<br />Konstabel Els fights with the Doberman of the house and eventually kills him, considering the difficult battle, he wants to stuff the animal, that would end up in one of the offices.<br /><br />Phenomenal humor is mixed with appalling, terrible acts and attitudes- the Kommandant would rather shoot and kill Miss Hazlestone than allow her story of sex with an African man reach others - the result is a fascinating, hilarious, educational, fabulous book.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9644313019406033922" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2457194398" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2457194398" />
    <a title="Glen Engel-Cox" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1028300-glen-engel-cox"><img alt="Glen Engel-Cox" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1468694308p2/1028300.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2457194398?book_show_action=true">Jul 14, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Glen Engel-Cox" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Glen Engel-Cox" href="/user/show/1028300-glen-engel-cox">Glen Engel-Cox</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2457194398" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer377899612312053699"><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1398734.David_Baboulene' title='David Baboulene' rel='nofollow noopener'>David Baboulene</a> (a friend in Britain) and I sent each other some favorite books recently. Since David hadn’t read much SF/F, I sent him <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/23704.Jonathan_Carroll' title='Jonathan Carroll' rel='nofollow noopener'>Jonathan Carroll</a>’s <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/42146.Bones_of_the_Moon__Answered_Prayers___1_' title='Bones of the Moon (Answered Prayers, #1) by Jonathan Carroll' rel='nofollow noopener'>Bones of the Moon</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/86475.James_P__Blaylock' title='James P. Blaylock' rel='nofollow noopener'>James P. Blaylock</a>’s <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/430871.The_Last_Coin__Christian_Trilogy___1_' title='The Last Coin (Christian Trilogy, #1) by James P. Blaylock' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Last Coin</a>. In turn, he sent me some British humor: <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/33017.Tom_Sharpe' title='Tom Sharpe' rel='nofollow noopener'>Tom Sharpe</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/153571.Clive_James' title='Clive James' rel='nofollow noopener'>Clive James</a>. James’ books were quite interesting–a well-written autobiography with some sly touches that never quite had me belly-laughing, but kept me reading. Sharpe, on the other hand, I fell into with a gusto. From page one of <b>Ri</b></span>
  <span id="freeText377899612312053699" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1398734.David_Baboulene" title="David Baboulene" rel="nofollow noopener">David Baboulene</a> (a friend in Britain) and I sent each other some favorite books recently. Since David hadn’t read much SF/F, I sent him <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/23704.Jonathan_Carroll" title="Jonathan Carroll" rel="nofollow noopener">Jonathan Carroll</a>’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/42146.Bones_of_the_Moon__Answered_Prayers___1_" title="Bones of the Moon (Answered Prayers, #1) by Jonathan Carroll" rel="nofollow noopener">Bones of the Moon</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/86475.James_P__Blaylock" title="James P. Blaylock" rel="nofollow noopener">James P. Blaylock</a>’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/430871.The_Last_Coin__Christian_Trilogy___1_" title="The Last Coin (Christian Trilogy, #1) by James P. Blaylock" rel="nofollow noopener">The Last Coin</a>. In turn, he sent me some British humor: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/33017.Tom_Sharpe" title="Tom Sharpe" rel="nofollow noopener">Tom Sharpe</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/153571.Clive_James" title="Clive James" rel="nofollow noopener">Clive James</a>. James’ books were quite interesting–a well-written autobiography with some sly touches that never quite had me belly-laughing, but kept me reading. Sharpe, on the other hand, I fell into with a gusto. From page one of <b>Riotous Assembly</b>, my hands were doing double-duty turning pages and trying to keep my sides from splitting.<br /><br />Imagine the writer you would get if you mixed <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7963.P_G__Wodehouse" title="P.G. Wodehouse" rel="nofollow noopener">P. G. Wodehouse</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5237.Hunter_S__Thompson" title="Hunter S. Thompson" rel="nofollow noopener">Hunter S. Thompson</a>, and then placed them in South Africa; that’s Tom Sharpe. He indeed manages to combine the wit and language skills, as well as the awkward situations, of Wodehouse with the sharpened pen of satire and low opinion of humans from Thompson, and his target is South Africa and the police forces there (I believe that he was jailed there for awhile, and ultimately deported).<br /><br />Upon finishing <b>Riotous Assembly</b>, I rushed to see if I could find any more by this Sharpe fellow. Luckily, Vintage has brought him across the sea for our enjoyment. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/58514.Indecent_Exposure" title="Indecent Exposure by Tom Sharpe" rel="nofollow noopener">Indecent Exposure</a> is the sequel to <b>Riotous Assembly</b> and just as funny; perhaps even funnier, given the satire of the Dornford Yates club (a group of Englishmen who adore the veddy British writer Dornford Yates who is clearly an analog for Wodehouse) within the larger South African satire. I also read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/420966.Wilt__Wilt___1_" title="Wilt (Wilt, #1) by Tom Sharpe" rel="nofollow noopener">Wilt</a>, in which he drops some of the satirical and plays the perverted Wodehouse more. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/420966.Wilt__Wilt___1_" title="Wilt (Wilt, #1) by Tom Sharpe" rel="nofollow noopener">Wilt</a> is okay, but I would suggest you try the South African novels first. If you’re like me, you’ll have to read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/420966.Wilt__Wilt___1_" title="Wilt (Wilt, #1) by Tom Sharpe" rel="nofollow noopener">Wilt</a> or any of his other novels then–just because you can’t get enough of this amazing fellow.</span>
  <a data-text-id="377899612312053699" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2474748915" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2474748915" />
    <a title="Roland" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1850691-roland"><img alt="Roland" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1358425416p2/1850691.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2474748915?book_show_action=true">Jul 29, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Roland" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Roland" href="/user/show/1850691-roland">Roland</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer2474748915&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2474748915" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3227869916661336338">Warning: This isn‘t funny. Only, it‘s hilarious for quite a while, but eventually, it all sticks in your throat. You see, there‘s only so much you can say about nincompoop afrikander apartheid policemen and stay humorous. At the end of the day (roughly in 1991, at last) it just wasn‘t funny, was it? This novel might make anyone think twice about the merits of the TRC (Truth and Reconciliation Commission – the agency which tried to get South Africa into the modern age without getting a tenth of t</span>
  <span id="freeText3227869916661336338" style="display:none">Warning: This isn‘t funny. Only, it‘s hilarious for quite a while, but eventually, it all sticks in your throat. You see, there‘s only so much you can say about nincompoop afrikander apartheid policemen and stay humorous. At the end of the day (roughly in 1991, at last) it just wasn‘t funny, was it? This novel might make anyone think twice about the merits of the TRC (Truth and Reconciliation Commission – the agency which tried to get South Africa into the modern age without getting a tenth of the white population punished). And nobody in their right minds (and I don‘t mean ‚right‘ to mean ‚politically right‘, because that‘s not where I am) would want to think twice about the TRC. At the end of the day – and the novel – this just isn‘t funny. Sharpe is unbelievably sharp linguistically – I‘d rate him up there with T.C. Boyle (am not joking here) – but it all spins round and round and doesn‘t stop. For those who have read (spoiler alert, if you must know): No fun should be contrived from characters like Konstabel Els. Konstabel Els is not funny. He – or his likes – existed in die Republiek van Suid-Afrika. That is not a joke. No fun can be made of Adolf Eichmann or Josef Mengele. And no: It‘s not too much to compare these to Els and his likes.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3227869916661336338" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('05zihz8Z5nOEYsxSO3jqHw7R1YnOahq6I/8Zch9RlYz9oLy8v1Irw8KSHUYjF34xtpUGPnrw5zcee6fxxJElMQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

In collections such as Will You Please Be Quiet, Please? and What We Talk About When We Talk About<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText14937084937046448514\" style=\"display:none\">Raymond Carver’s spare dramas of loneliness, despair, and troubled relationships breathed new life into the American short story of the 1970s and ’80s. In collections such as Will You Please Be Quiet, Please? and What We Talk About When We Talk About Love, Carver wrote with unflinching exactness about men and women enduring lives on the knife-edge of poverty and other deprivations. Beneath his pared-down surfaces run disturbing, violent undercurrents. Suggestive rather than explicit, and seeming all the more powerful for what is left unsaid, Carver’s stories were held up as exemplars of a new school in American fiction known as minimalism or “dirty realism,” a movement whose wide influence continues to this day. Carver’s stories were brilliant in their detachment and use of the oblique, ambiguous gesture, yet there were signs of a different sort of sensibility at work. In books such as Cathedral and the later tales included in the collected stories volume Where I’m Calling From, Carver revealed himself to be a more expansive writer than in the earlier published books, displaying Chekhovian sympathies toward his characters and relying less on elliptical effects.\n\nIn gathering all of Carver’s stories, including early sketches and posthumously discovered works, The Library of America’s Collected Stories provides a comprehensive overview of Carver’s career as we have come to know it: the promise of Will You Please Be Quiet, Please? and the breakthrough of What We Talk About, on through the departures taken in Cathedral and the pathos of the late stories. But it also prompts a fresh consideration of Carver by presenting Beginners, an edition of the manuscript of What We Talk About When We Talk About Love that Carver submitted to Gordon Lish, his editor and a crucial influence on his development. Lish’s editing was so extensive that at one point Carver wrote him an anguished letter asking him not to publish the book; now, for the first time, readers can read both the manuscript and published versions of the collection that established Carver as a major American writer. Offering a fascinating window into the complex, fraught relationship between writer and editor, Beginners expands our sense of Carver and is essential reading for anyone who cares about his <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer11740270038020103040">Tom Sharpe was an English satirical author, born in London and educated at Lancing College and at Pembroke College, Cambridge. After National Service with the Royal Marines he moved to South Africa in 1951, doing social work and teaching in Natal, until deported in 1961.<br/><br/>His work in South Africa inspired the novels Riotous Assembly and Indecent Exposure. From 1963 until 1972 he was a History lectur</span>
  <span id="freeText11740270038020103040" style="display:none">Tom Sharpe was an English satirical author, born in London and educated at Lancing College and at Pembroke College, Cambridge. After National Service with the Royal Marines he moved to South Africa in 1951, doing social work and teaching in Natal, until deported in 1961.<br /><br />His work in South Africa inspired the novels Riotous Assembly and Indecent Exposure. From 1963 until 1972 he was a History lecturer at the Cambridge College of Arts and Technology, which inspired his "Wilt" series <i>Wilt</i>, <i>The Wilt Alternative</i>, <i>Wilt on High</i> and <i>Wilt in Nowhere</i>.<br /><br />His novels feature bitter and outrageous satire of the apartheid regime (<i>Riotous Assembly</i> and its sequel <i>Indecent Exposure</i>), dumbed- or watered-down education (the <i>Wilt</i> series), English class snobbery (<i>Ancestral Vices</i>, <i>Porterhouse Blue</i>, <i>Grantchester Grind</i>), the literary world (<i>The Great Pursuit</i>), political extremists of all stripes, political correctness, bureaucracy and stupidity in general. Characters may indulge in bizarre sexual practices, and coarser characters use very graphic and/or profane language in dialogue. Sharpe often parodies the language and style of specific authors commonly associated with the social group held up for ridicule. Sharpe's bestselling books have been translated into many languages.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11740270038020103040" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
